This error led to a subjective and problematic evaluation of bids by the bid assessment and evaluation committee and the CEO.
The unsuccessful tenderer contested the results of the procurement procedure.
A subsequent investigation by the Office for the Protection of Competition led to the contract for the construction contract being signed later than originally planned.
This meant that the 2004 summer construction season, after bids were evaluated, was wasted and construction time was reduced.
The construction company agreed to build the bridge in a shorter period of time, but at the cost of a substantial increase in costs.
The procedure offered included the double installation of the bridge top, a change in the construction of the pillars and the installation and equipment of the assembly areas.
In addition, the construction company was not able to procure the originally designed type of steel within the necessary time.
The new type of steel - at a price higher by about €4 million, which had to be paid in full by the SDD - made it possible to reduce the time for the implementation of anti-corrosion protection, which in turn made it possible to reduce the total construction time of the border bridge.
Compared to the net price of €18.3 million offered in the tender, the total cost of the construction of the border bridge will increase considerably as a result of the accelerated procedure and other adjustments in funding.
The reduced time allocated to construction work has led to a price increase of about €5.9 million.
The net cost is currently around €25.9 million, without taking into account automatic price increases (as of October 2006).
Comparison of prices of bridges and tunnels
The two supreme audit institutions analysed the prices of bridges on the D 8 and A 17 motorways in order to identify bridges whose price was too high and to compare the prices of bridges on the German and Czech sides.
The analysis was based on mathematical statistical methods.
In addition, the tunnels on D 8 were compared with similar tunnels on motorways in Germany (A 71, A 38 and A 4).
There are no similar tunnels on the A17.
In the preparation of this part of the inspection, it was important to identify precisely the relevant prices.
The two supreme audit institutions have set the following principles for comparing the prices of bridges: Based on the actual price paid for the bridge, excluding value added tax (VAT rates vary in the Czech Republic and the SNR and are subject to changes); do not take into account the prices for the special foundations of the bridge; recalculate the prices in CZK to €according to the rate of the European Central Bank applicable on 30 December 2005 (CZK 29 = €1).
On both D 8 and A 17 there are bridges of different structures (steel concrete, prestressed reinforced concrete, coupled steel and steel) and of different sizes.
In order to ensure the homogeneity of the structures examined, the bridges were divided into groups according to the type of structure.
Within these groups, the size of the bridge was also taken into account in the analyses.
Concrete bridges were by far the most common type of construction on both sides of the border (20 bridges on D 8, 36 bridges on A 17).
The analysis focused mainly on this group of bridges, as the size of the population allowed the use of mathematical and statistical methods with a high degree of reliability of the results.
The inspections found that prices per m 2 of concrete bridges on both D 8 and A 17 depend on the size of the bridge (its length, area): The larger the bridge, the lower the price per m2 of bridge construction.
For German bridges, the price's dependence on size was stronger than for Czech bridges (see graphs below).
Two concrete bridges on A 17 and four on D 8 have been identified, the price of which is significantly higher.
In order to find these bridges, a method was used to detect atypical values in a set of numbers and a regression analysis taking into account the dependence of the price of the bridge on its size (the distance from the regression curve upwards is decisive for determining atypical expensive bridges).
However, information on the atypical high price of these bridges should only be considered as an indicator of which bridges need to be targeted.
In order to draw a generally applicable conclusion, the reasons for the high price in each case identified should be analysed.
One such cause may be inefficient administrative measures, or environmental protection requirements or any other acceptable reason.
In view of the fact that Czech concrete bridges are on average longer than German bridges (see table below), the proven negative dependence of the bridge price on its length could lead to the assumption that Czech bridges should be cheaper.
The hypothesis of lower prices of Czech concrete bridges is also supported by lower labour costs in the construction sector in the Czech Republic compared to Germany.
On the contrary, the results of the analysis show that the prices of concrete bridges on D 8 are higher than on A 17.
In the course of the joint negotiations, two possible causes of this situation were identified:
1.
In Germany, most bridges are awarded as separate contracts.
Such an award allows small and medium-sized regional firms to take part in the competition for the contract.
In the Czech Republic, many bridges are awarded as part of the contract for part of the motorway route, which is several kilometres long.
Small and medium-sized companies do not participate in such a competition because the contract is too big for them.
2.
In the Czech Republic, there is more variability in the construction of bridges than in Germany.
In this context, the SAO found that the type of construction of the bridge is decided long before its construction.
The decision is taken at the time when the application for a territorial decision is submitted by the SDD.
At this time, the SDD selects types of bridges from several options, but economy plays a smaller role in the decision.
A general conclusion on the prices of bridges in the Czech Republic and Germany could only be drawn on the basis of a thorough analysis of the construction market in both countries, which would, however, go beyond the scope of this inspection.
The Boundary Bridge is the most expensive combined steel concrete bridge on D 8 and A 17.
In the case of tunnels, the prices of tunnels on D 8 were found to be twice as high as those in Germany.
However, a comparison of the main construction-technical parameters of the tunnels on D 8 with the tunnels in Germany did not show any significant difference.
Significant price differences could be attributed to the fact that there is no real competition in the construction of tunnels in the Czech Republic.
However, the small number of tunnels did not make it possible to draw generally applicable conclusions.
Effectiveness and efficiency of the construction of the Dresden - Prague motorway
The independent Czech Republic was established in 1993.
In the same year, the government approved a concept for the development of the motorway network.
The D8 motorway was to be gradually extended from Prague to the state border with Germany.
Section 0805 through the Czech Central Mountains should have been acquired before the border section 0807.
The Czech Republic procures the D 8 motorway as part of its programme funding.
The programme No 327 220 "Construction of the D 8 motorway Prague - Ústí nad Labem - state border of the Czech Republic / Germany" set out two objectives for the construction of the D 8 motorway:
1.
Improve the connection between Prague and Ústí nad Labem - a national objective;
2.
Improve connections between Prague and Berlin in the framework of the 4th Trans-European Transport Corridor Berlin-Istanbul - an international goal.
According to the programme, the construction of the section running through the Czech Central Mountains was to be completed in June 2008 and the construction of the border section 0807 in June 2007.
It should be added here that the completion date is not the same as the commissioning date.
The SAO found that the preparation of the construction of the section running through the Czech Central Mountains is now at the stage of an official planning decision.
Environmental activists have challenged the decision with lawsuits.
There is no final decision on the actions.
It cannot therefore be ruled out that the section running through the Czech Central Mountains will be completed several years later than stated in the documentation of the programme.
In the summer of 2006, the Czech Ministry of Transport proposed to postpone the completion date of section 0805 to 2010.
As a result of these facts, the construction of the border section did not achieve the national goal of improving the connection between Prague and Ústí nad Labem.
The SDD carried out an estimate of the capital expenditure for the border section D 8 no. 0807 and the section no. 0805 through the Bohemian Central Mountains in the years 1999-2001.
The estimated capital expenditure included in the investment costs is about €639 million for both sections 0805 and 0807.
Table 2: Results of the economic assessment (EIRR)
All motorways and expressways under consideration are open, both sections of D 8 opened in 2007.
On the northern part of the border section, traffic intensity of 18,000 vehicles per day in 2010
All motorways and expressways under consideration are open, both sections of D 8 opened in 2007.
On the northern part of the border section, traffic intensity of 22 000 vehicles per day in 2010
All motorways and expressways under consideration are open, both sections of D 8 opened in 2007.
On the northern part of the border section, traffic intensity of 25 000 vehicles per day in 2010
The economic analysis carried out in 2001 was based on the assumption that the border section and the section through the Czech Central Mountains would be completed in time.
At the time of the inspection, however, it is clear that the section through the Czech Central Mountains will not be opened on the original date.
This will reduce the above EIRR values.
The cost of both sections, net of VAT, calculated as at 31 December 2005, was €897 million, i.e. higher by 27.4% than the cost level used in the economic analysis in 2001.
However, there is a risk that section 0805 will run on a different, more expensive route, which would affect the above-mentioned threshold of economic viability.
Economic analysis has shown that the construction of both sections of motorway is economically viable with a traffic intensity of at least 18 000 vehicles per day in 2010.
The forecast for 2008 was 22,940 vehicles, but a study addressing the issue of bypass routes for the missing section through the Bohemian Central Mountains projects a traffic intensity of 12,600 to 13,750 vehicles per day, pushing the investment below the limit of economic viability.
There is a high probability of congestion and thus an increase in external costs, which were not foreseen in the original economic analysis, due to the higher load on the bypass routes for the missing section through the Czech Central Mountains.
The aforementioned reduction in revenues and increase in costs means that the already low EIRR that the construction of the border section D 8 was expected to have will not be achieved.
Construction of the D 8 border section is before inefficent.
The construction of the A 17 motorway also resulted in cost overruns similar to those of the two sections on the D 8.
As part of the overall economic assessment of the Federal Road Development Plan for long-distance transport for 1992, the Federal Ministry of Transport of Germany estimated the cost to be around €320 million.
The resulting cost-benefit ratio was 4.6.
In the meantime, however, costs have risen sharply to around €670 million, reducing the cost-benefit ratio by more than half.
In addition to a parallel audit carried out jointly with the SAO, BRH audited the preparation and construction of the A17 motorway in 2003 and 2005.
In doing so, the BRH found that the competent approval state authority had requested an off-road tunnel for Section A 17, which was not needed.
During the inspection, BRH was able to convince the Federal Ministry and the German Road Administration that the tunnel was not necessary.
This resulted in savings of around €10 million in construction costs alone and €0.15 million in annual operating costs.
In its assessment of the first section A 17, BRH found that the construction project had not been properly prepared.
This is illustrated by the following example:
At the preparatory stage, the Road Administration did not carry out a survey of the conditions of the bedrock in the Dölzschen rocks, as the site was difficult to access.
This was carried out only after the tunnel stamping machine hit the rock.
Tests have shown that the rocks inside are more weathered than expected during the selection process.
As a result, costly measures such as anchoring, injection and stone-fall protection nets had to be taken, costing about €3.3 million.
The Roads and Motorways Directorate had to bear the costs not only of other emergency operations, such as safety measures in the Dölzschen rocks, but also of measures that would not have been necessary if the construction project had been properly prepared first.
Expenditure of this kind includes costs related to the interruption of construction operations (€3.2 million), additional training of drilling personnel (€0.5 million) and rising wages and goods costs due to project overruns (€7 million).
The cost overruns thus amounted to around €10.7 million, which was not matched by any added value.
On the basis of the lessons learned from this and other audited projects, BRH asked the Federal Ministry to apply stricter guidance in its supervision of road construction works.
In doing so, the Federal Ministry should give the Road and Motorway Directorates the necessary incentive to implement quality assurance systems.
Benefits of cooperation for BRH and SAO Cooperation fulfilled the intention of the two audit institutions.
BRH gained control knowledge of the preparation, assignment and progress of the construction of the border bridge.
The two supreme audit institutions have built a database on the price of bridges and tunnels, which will be used for future inspections.
The SAO found that the main construction-technical parameters of bridges, tunnels and the route of the D 8 motorway do not differ from those of the A 17.
The auditors of both institutions involved in the cooperation have gained new experience, which they can use for further inspections.

Coordinated control of funds earmarked for the financing of projects implemented under the Operational Programme for Cross-Border Cooperation Czech Republic - Republic of Poland 2007-2013
Introduction
The developing community of European citizens is the driving force behind cross-border cooperation as an integral part of the wider territorial cooperation that has been part of our lives for the past 20 years.
So let this community give an opening statement on this joint report by the two supreme audit institutions of neighbouring countries:
"185 million EU citizens (or 37% of the population) live in border areas.
These areas are usually underdeveloped and peripheral territories, often a remnant of darker parts of Europe's history.
The main objective of cross-border cooperation is to help mitigate the existence of borders as administrative, legal and physical barriers and to further develop the dormant potential of border regions.
The programmes cover different border regions: Some cover maritime areas, others inland borders within the EU or borders shared with candidate countries.
Most of the programmes are bilateral, but some involve more than two neighbouring countries. "
The programmes last for seven years and receive a total of €5.7 billion from the EU budget.
"The programmes cover the following activities: Repairs and reconstruction of cross-border roads, cycle paths and bridges, investment in cross-border waste management systems, health facilities, research centres, flood control measures, etc., joint management of natural sites or development of tourist destinations, development of common services for the local population, employment advice, creation of thematic networks and clusters for innovation.
Support from the programme is provided on the condition that organisations from both sides of the border (such as regional authorities, universities or small and medium-sized enterprises) come together to implement projects that are based on the needs of the border region.
Representatives of the cooperating regions, relevant ministries and other local partners meet regularly to decide which projects will be supported.
This system should lead to a sustainable form of cooperation even after the programme itself has ended.
Funding shall be provided on condition that project partners from both sides of the border cooperate in a way that fulfils at least two of the following four characteristics: Joint preparation, joint implementation, joint staff, joint financing. "
Within the partnership, the tasks and responsibilities for the preparation, implementation, financing and control of project activities are clearly defined and allocated to the relevant partners.
The main attributes of the partnership include: Joint selection of the lead partner, joint preparation of the project and the application, conclusion of a joint partnership agreement signed by all partners, taking overall responsibility for the implementation of the project by the lead partner, total support payments are received by the lead partner and transferred to the relevant project partners.
Eligible projects carried out on the territory of regions and provinces adjacent to the common border may be supported.
Objectives AND SCOPE OF CONTROL
On 11 December 2007, the European Commission (EC) approved the Operational Programme for Cross-Border Cooperation Czech Republic - Republic of Poland 2007-2013 (Opps ČR-POL).
€258,187,467 has been earmarked for the implementation of the operational programme.
The total amount included a contribution from European funds (ERDF) totalling €219,459,344 (85%), which must be used up by the end of 2015.
Financial data on the implementation of EU funds from the Operational Programme for Cross-Border Cooperation Czech Republic - Republic of Poland 2007-2013 at the end of 2013 are presented in the following table:
The agreement on cooperation between the supreme audit institutions of the Czech Republic (SAO) and the Republic of Poland (NIK) was concluded by the presidents of both offices on 27 February 2013 after previous mutual discussions at the meeting of the heads of the supreme audit institutions of the Visegrad Four, Austria and Slovenia, which took place in September 2012 in Lovasberény, Hungary.
The aim of the cooperation was to check whether the implementation of the operational programme was carried out in accordance with legislation and with binding programming documents or agreements, whether the management and control system was functioning reliably, whether the objectives of the operational programme were being achieved and whether the selected projects were being carried out in accordance with the conditions laid down for the support granted.
This cooperation took the form of a parallel check, in which the supervisory authorities independently examined, on the basis of their legal mandate, topics the content of which had been discussed jointly in advance.
ThissegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
The audit also included elements of a performance audit.
The selected topics were examined on a sample of projects with project partners on both sides of the border.
As a result of the checks carried out, the audit findings were published in the usual way by both authorities.
On the basis of the two control conclusions set out in the Annex, this joint report has been produced.
The selected joint control sample included six projects (Annex 1) which fall under aid area 1.1 - Strengthening transport accessibility and aid area 2.2 - Promoting tourism development.
Prior to the start of the audit, the disbursement of funds from these two support areas represented more than 60% of the funds paid to beneficiaries by the end of 2012 from the ERDF for all implemented projects across Opps ČR-POL.
ThissegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
In addition to the audit of a joint sample of six projects, the SAO audited another 13 projects and NIK another two (all in the field of support 1.1 and 2.2).
The funds paid to the ERDF beneficiaries, which were audited by the SAO and NIK, amounted to a total of €25,362,174 (SAO) and €8,450,784 (NIK).
The audited amount represented 41% of the total amount paid to beneficiaries in aid areas 1.1 and 2.2.
Main CONTROL CONCLUSIONS
Both audits carried out independently by the SAO on the Czech side and the NIK on the Polish side showed that the level of irregularities identified did not exceed the level of significance.
The implementation of Opps ČR-POL, including the functioning of the management and control system and the implementation of selected projects, was successful.
The audited entities, which were part of the management and control system, took steps to reduce the risk of misapplication of funds and non-use of ERDF funds.
Evidence of the effectiveness of the projects carried out under the operational programme is the high level of achievement of the objectives set, which was also supported by the results of a survey of the social evaluation of the implementation of the projects.
Summary OF THE RESULTS OF CONTROL
A. Evaluation of the management and control system
The coordinated approach made it possible to check the management and control system of Opps ČR-POL implemented by the following institutions in both countries: The managing authority (Ministry of Regional Development of the Czech Republic), the national coordinator (Ministry of Regional Development POL) and the auditors (Centre for Regional Development and Voivodship).
At the same time, the coordinated audit made it possible to evaluate the tasks and functions carried out by the Joint Technical Secretariat and the Monitoring Committee.
During the evaluation of the management system, the audit focused on the activities carried out in relation to the setting up and performance of the functions of the managing authority and the national coordinator, in particular in relation to the setting of the objectives of the programme and the monitoring of progress made, evaluation activities and annual reports, the assessment, evaluation, selection and approval of project applications, the absorption of programme funds and the fulfilment of the obligation to publish correct, complete and up-to-date information on subsidies, the control activities of the managing authority, the setting up of the performance of activities and outputs from audits of the entrusted audit body and the system for reporting and monitoring irregularities and its compliance, while at the same time the coordination of tasks and cooperation between the institutions involved in Opps ČR-POL was examined.
In addition, the activities of the competent authorities in the area of risk analysis and the drawing up of control plans at the site of implementation of projects were examined and the evaluations of project applications carried out by independent evaluators were assessed.
The examination of the control system included a review of administrative and on-the-spot checks, which allowed for the approval of e.g. supplies of goods and co-financed services, the eligibility of expenditure submitted for reimbursement, as well as the compliance of the disbursement of these funds and related activities (or parts thereof) with national and EU legislation.
Significant shortcomings were identified by the SAO audit in the area of publishing correct, complete and up-to-date information about the subsidies provided.
In the area of project evaluation and approval, a risk of lack of transparency in this process has been identified.
Further weaknesses were found in the setting of monitoring indicators and in the setting of the irregularity reporting system.
On the basis of these findings, the Supreme Audit Office assessed the control and management system implemented by the Managing Authority as partially effective.
The irregularities identified by the NIK, which related to the functioning of the management and control system in Poland in relation to violations of applicable laws and rules, were not considered significant.
These irregularities only had an impact on individual projects and did not have a significant impact on the implementation of the entire Opps ČR-POL.
Despite the identified irregularities, the NIK assessed the functioning of the system positively.
Evaluation of the results of projects
The audit did not show a discrepancy between the indicators and the objectives set for each project and those defined by the operational programme.
In all the projects audited, the expected results were achieved, the funded activities were implemented and the indicators (measurable parameters) set out in the project applications of the operational programme were met.
The audit conclusions are in line with the result of the survey of the social evaluation of the implementation of the projects described below.
An important aspect that the SAO and NIK paid attention to in their conclusions is the limited possibility of an objective evaluation of the implementation of the expected results of individual projects with regard to the objectives of individual projects and at the same time the objectives of the entire operational programme.
On the initiative of the Supreme Audit Chamber, a public opinion poll was conducted in the summer of 2013 at the site of the implementation of projects selected for the joint control sample.
The survey and its evaluation were carried out by the School of Higher Vocational Education in Nysa (Poland).
In agreement with the Supreme Audit Office, the investigation was carried out on both sides of the border between 2,820 respondents, both for the public in Poland in relation to the part of the project carried out by Polish project partners and for the Czech public in relation to the part of the project carried out in the Czech Republic.
The survey covered in particular the suitability, quality, exchange of information, use of resources and social function of the projects, including the investments made.
The number of respondents per project is set out in Annex 2.
The assessment of the survey responses indicated that the overall social evaluation of the projects was good, reaching 4.8 points (on a scale with a maximum of 8 points), meaning that the results of the projects were viewed positively by the majority of respondents, both with regard to the objectives and other parameters set out in the project applications.
According to the respondents, the projects examined met the criteria set out in the operational programme.
Respondents also noted the positive impact of investments on improving the quality of transport and tourism infrastructure, accessibility, tourist attractiveness, business conditions, quality of life of citizens and the renewal of contacts between residents of the border region.
On the other hand, the overall assessment of the responses indicated some difference between the actual level of achievement of the objectives / parameters (actual level of impact) and the level expected by the respondents.
This difference relates to the evaluation of the results of individual projects, where in some cases there is a significant difference.
The results of the survey were in line with the views of participants in the panel discussions (target groups) carried out in the framework of the NIK inspection on the Polish side, who pointed out the need to implement the projects and confirmed their positive public acceptance and the fulfilment of the expected results.
C. Evaluation of the legality of the audited projects
The beneficiaries were checked for compliance of the material and time implementation of the project with the schedule and content of the project application, the fulfilment of the stated objectives and purpose of the project, the fulfilment of binding monitoring indicators, the management of changes in the projects, the handling of any project revenue, the fulfilment of the obligation to keep separate accounts of project transactions, compliance with the ban on financing projects from other EU sources, the implementation of the project partnership and compliance with its conditions, the fulfilment of mandatory publicity concerning in particular the dissemination of awareness of the sources of financing of projects from the EU, compliance with the conditions of eligibility of project expenditure, the selection of contractors and the verification of the deliverables on the basis of public contracts awarded.
The total financial value of the shortcomings of the qualified SAO and NIK as expenditure ineligible for EU funding was found to be €85,094.
However, this amount did not exceed the established level of materiality related to the total amount of funds audited for the sample of projects.
Despite the irregularities found, the SAO and NIK evaluated the implementation of the selected projects positively by the beneficiaries.
In most cases, the commitments under the terms of the aid were duly fulfilled by the beneficiaries.
The funds earmarked for the implementation of the audited projects were spent in accordance with EU regulations and national law.
However, seven out of the eight beneficiaries audited by the NIK were found to be in breach of their material and financial obligations.
These were individual cases of a non-systemic nature and related in particular to the inconsistent preparation of project documentation and breaches of public procurement rules, which did not, however, affect the choice of contractor or the supply of contractually agreed works.
In only one of the projects audited by the NIK can an irregularity found relating to non-compliance with reporting obligations be classified as ineligible expenditure, amounting to PLN 43 003 (of which EUR 8 231 financed by the ERDF).
On the other side of the border, in three of the 19 projects audited, the SAO identified ineligible expenditure amounting to CZK 2,098,053 (of which €76,863 financed by the ERDF).
Irregularities included the failure to deliver paid materials and works, or the cost of upgrading a property owned by a person who had no connection to the project either as a beneficiary or as a project partner.
Recommendations
The joint recommendation of the SAO and the NIK concerns the improvement of the monitoring system, especially with regard to the consistent establishment of proper monitoring indicators for all specific objectives of the priority axis of the operational programme.
In general, efforts should also be made to establish indicators at programme and project level that allow for more reliable verification of the implementation of the identified impacts.
The notes and recommendations resulting from the checks carried out on both sides of the border are included in the separate audit conclusions of the SAO and NIK on CD.
Communiqué
The report was written in Czech, English and Polish and signed by the presidents of the supreme audit institutions of the Czech Republic and the Republic of Poland.
Public works procurement and prevention of corruption
Joint report on the parallel audit carried out by the Czech Supreme Audit Office (SAO) and the German Federal Court of Auditors (BRH)
Introduction and overview
Public procurement of goods and services by contracting authorities is very important for both the state and the private sector.
The role of the State is to use public resources as efficiently and economically as possible and to ensure fair and regulated free competition.
In the European Union (EU), public procurement is a key factor in the success of the Single Economic Area.
In its legislation, the EU has set limits beyond which contracting authorities must meet certain requirements to guarantee a broad and unrestricted competitive environment across the EU.
EU Member States must therefore transpose European procurement law into their national legislation and put it into practice.
Both the Czech Republic and Germany have already done so.
For public contracts that are below EU limits in value, the regulation of their award remains the responsibility of individual countries.
In addition, public procurement is prone to corruption, with a consequent high potential for harm.
Contracting authorities are therefore obliged to take reasonable measures in their procurement procedures to prevent corruption and unfair advantage (prevention of corruption).
In January 2011, the Supreme Audit Office of the Czech Republic (Czech SAI - SAO) and the Federal Court of Auditors of Germany (German SAI - BRH) agreed to carry out parallel inspections in the field of procurement procedures and prevention of corruption in land and transport buildings.
Cooperation was also focused on the area of European procurement law, as transposed into national legislation.
Controls included procurement below European limits to tackle the prevention of corruption.
The audit findings of the two supreme audit institutions are summarised in a joint report addressed, inter alia, to international organisations such as the International Organisation of Supreme Audit Institutions (INTOSAI).
Areas of control and controlled institutions
The BRH assessment is based on blanket inspections of the above mentioned areas, which were carried out with the German building authorities responsible for federal transport and land construction between 2011 and 2012.
For example, the construction of motorways and large-scale building projects were evaluated.
The BRH also included findings from past inspections.
The SAO's assessment is based on inspections carried out between 2008 and 2012, which focused on the construction of roads, railway lines, water projects, large buildings (hospitals, cultural facilities, universities) and some other buildings in the Czech Republic.
In Germany, the award of federal public contracts is mostly carried out by the regional building authorities.
In the Czech Republic, public procurement in the field of transport infrastructure is the responsibility of the state institutions for road and rail construction.
In the field of civil engineering, public procurement is carried out by entities receiving budget funds.
In both countries, the services of external experts are also used at different stages of the procurement process.
The evaluation should take into account the differences in public procurement legislation and the specificities of the construction market in both countries.
Structure of the Public Procurement Act in both countries
In Germany, the conduct of European procurement procedures is governed by laws and regulations.
Procurement procedures limited to Germany are governed by budgetary law and the relevant administrative acts.
The internal administrative rules contain numerous additional provisions for both European and national procurement procedures.
Further details on the requirements of European procurement law and the prevention of corruption are set out in the so-called federal government procurement manuals, in which the requirements are elaborated or formulated in greater detail in the light of legal precedents in the field of procurement law.
In the Czech Republic, public procurement is governed by the Public Procurement Act and other legislation relating to contracts with a value above and below European limits.
The procurement law underwent significant changes in 2012.
The new rules have strengthened the transparency of procurement procedures, opened the way for more competition and reduced opportunities for corruption in the procurement process.
Fundamental aspects of procurement law in both countries
As far as European procurement procedures are concerned, open and restricted procedures in particular need to be applied in both countries.
In Germany, contracts below the European threshold are awarded in accordance with national rules (public call for competition, restricted call for tender, negotiated procedure), but these are practically identical to the above conditions for European procurement procedures.
Public procurement in Germany is characterised by the principle of preference for open procedures (where candidates are invited on a European scale) and public calls for participation (only in the case of national procedures).
In open procedures and in the event of a call for competition, any candidate may submit a tender.
Another important feature of German procurement law is the support of small and medium-sized companies.
This is mainly achieved by dividing the total public contract into commercially specific parts and dividing it into sub-contracts.
The award of contracts divided into these lots gives as many candidates as possible the opportunity to participate in the procedure.
The two characteristics of German procurement law are time-tested tools for obtaining good value for money.
More than 330,000 construction companies are registered in Germany, with 1.9 million employees.
In the Czech Republic, the terms of European procedures must also be applied in cases of public procurement with a value below the EU limit.
An exception is the simplified sub-standard procedure, where the contracting authority can use less demanding procedures.
In accordance with European law, Czech procurement law includes the conditions under which the contract must be awarded in order to ensure competition and achieve a favourable price.
Current Czech legislation allows contracting authorities to choose between an open procedure and a restricted procedure.
There are 85,000 construction companies registered in the Czech Republic, of which about 1,900 have 20 or more employees.
Total volume of contracting authorities' public works contracts and share of different types of procedures in both countries
In 2011, the total volume of construction works in Germany amounted to EUR 7 675 billion.
Of which CZK 1,138 billion (15%) was the share of public contracts, which, in addition to contracts awarded by the federal government, included contracts awarded by individual Länder and municipalities.
The total volume of federal road and land construction projects (excluding the construction of railways and waterworks) amounted to CZK 212.5 billion.
In 2011, the composition of the federal contracts for land works and transport works was as follows: 80.7% open procedures and public calls; 12.7% restricted procedures and restricted calls;
6.6% of negotiated procedures and direct procurement; 33.2% is the share of European procurement.
The volume of public construction contracts in the Czech Republic reached CZK 118.6 billion in 2011, which was about 25% of the total volume of all construction works.
In 2009, it reached CZK 194.0 billion, which was 37% of the total volume.
Structure of procurement types in 2011: 65.1% open procedures; 14.3% restricted procedures; 9.5% negotiated procedures; 8.4% simplified procedures for sub-standard contracts; 2.7% other procedures.
These figures include all public supply, service and works contracts awarded by contracting authorities in 2011.
The figures show that: Parallel checks in both countries have focused on a substantial part of public procurement procedures; prevention of corruption, which must always be taken into account in procurement procedures, has a significant financial impact; open significance.
Summary of the main results
The SAO and BRH carried out parallel checks on the award of European works contracts, with an emphasis on the prevention of corruption in their countries.
The inspections focused on comparing the legislation in both countries and the EU and on the implementation of public contracts in the field of land and transport construction.
Key findings are set out below:
The EU Public Procurement Directive has been transposed into the national laws of both countries (points 1 and 3.1).
Preventing corruption is not only the aim of the EU directive, but of the two countries' national procurement rules, which differ in part in terms of focus and detail.
In both countries, the basic requirement that tender documents must meet is to include all the information necessary for the preparation of tenders.
The specification shall be neutral and shall not contain any requirements restricting competition.
The tender documents in Germany generally meet these requirements.
In the Czech Republic, the problem is the lack of definition of the subject of the contract.
In Germany, it is required to divide contracts into lots where possible in order to promote a competitive environment.
According to BRH's findings, this requirement is respected in Germany.
The SAO's audits showed that in the Czech Republic, very large public works in the field of road construction are awarded without being divided into parts, or divided into parts, but only into very significant parts in volume. This method can distort competition and lead to restrictions on only a few large companies.
Such restrictions on market potential can lead to significant cost increases and risk unserious price negotiations and abusive agreements.
The legislation in both countries allows contracting authorities, under certain conditions, to award additional works if the need arises after the conclusion of the contract (point 4.1).
In both countries, the requirements for the correct identification of the type of procurement are met.
In the Czech Republic, according to the regulations in force, contracting entities are free to choose one of the open procurement procedures, namely an open procedure or a restricted procedure.
German procurement and budget law enshrines the primacy of open procedures for both European and national tenders, known as public calls for competition.
Open procurement procedures in both countries allow a larger pool of bidders to bid and also reduce the risk of corruption by making it more difficult to negotiate prices illegally between bidders.
At the same time, the best value for money results are being achieved.
In the case of large contracts whose estimated total price reaches or exceeds the limits set by the EU, the audited contracting authorities have always launched a European procurement procedure.
The correct determination of the estimated value of the contract plays a very important role in determining the type of procurement procedure.
In Germany, databases are used to objectively determine the price of public works, in which values are based on completed construction projects.
This is a sufficiently objective instrument.
In the Czech Republic, databases are used only in the field of road construction, but they are not yet an effective tool (point 4.2).
When awarding a public works contract in accordance with European procurement procedures, it is necessary to publish prior information and a contract notice, in order to strengthen competition by allowing candidates to prepare in time for the submission of tenders.
As a rule, the construction authorities audited completed with this requirement.
In isolated cases, errors in the information released, correct application of provisions and / or a lack of controls may have affected competition (item 4.3).
There is a greater risk of manipulation in the selection of candidates to participate in a restricted procurement procedure.
Under EU law, the number of candidates invited must be high enough to guarantee genuine competition.
The selection criteria shall be objective, non-discriminatory and published well in advance.
In addition to EU requirements, contracting authorities in both countries should rotate candidates as often as possible, inviting them to participate in the procedure when selecting suppliers.
In Germany, the requirements to prevent risks of manipulation are in part very detailed.
In some cases, the audited contracting entities did not comply with these requirements.
In the Czech Republic, selection decisions were not always credible (section 4.4).
In both countries, contracting authorities must ensure that all candidates wishing to submit a tender receive the same information in the course of the procurement procedure.
Any agreements between those interested parties and between them and third parties involved in the preparation of the specifications must, as far as possible, be prevented from restricting competition by means of unlawful agreements.
In Germany, the measures are more thorough than in the Czech Republic.
However, in several cases the German contracting authorities audited did not comply with this requirement (point 4.5).
In both countries, the principle of cross-checking and transparency must be applied when opening envelopes.
The requirements for labelling, documentation, protection and confidentiality of offers are designed to limit the scope for manipulation and are particularly detailed in Germany.
Checks in both countries found only a few cases of irregularities (section 4.6).
The assessment and evaluation of offers is regulated procedurally in both countries.
The relevant German measures are more comprehensive, but they have not always been respected by the audited contracting authorities.
For example, contracting entities submitted their tenders to third parties for inspection without prior assessment, without properly documenting any suspicious circumstances.
The bids were thus insufficiently protected against subsequent manipulation (point 4.7).
For reasons of legal protection, tenderers are required to be informed about the intended contract award before the latter is finalised.
This legal protection rule also is designed to prevent corrective action.
The law of the Czech Republic provides for judicial review of contract award procedures also for national tenders.
Only in few cases, the notification of the enterprises was not adequately supported (item 4.8).
The tender documentation for the audited contracting authorities in both countries was not always complete or fully credible.
In the case of several procurement procedures, the transparency requirements applied at European level were not always properly respected in the minutes of the procedure.
In addition, some of the weaknesses identified reduced the preventive anti-corruption function of the dossier.
Contracting authorities in both countries mostly complied with their reporting obligations (point 4.9).
Summary of the guiding principles of European procurement law and prevention of corruption
European procurement law
In order to ensure free competition in the EU, the European Parliament and the Council of the European Union adopted, inter alia, a Directive on the coordination of procedures for the award of public works contracts, public supply contracts and public service contracts with a view to ensuring the free movement of goods and services, and a Directive on the coordination of laws, regulations and administrative provisions relating to the application of review procedures to the award of public supply contracts and public works contracts (the Remedies Directive), both of which were to be transposed into national law.
The EU's general procurement principles include, in particular: The requirement of a competitive environment; the requirement of equal treatment or non-discrimination; the requirement of transparency.
EU procurement principles include The Directive on the coordination of public procurement procedures also includes provisions for construction projects with a total estimated value of €5 million or more.
These provisions affect: The content of the tender documents; the type of procurement procedure used; the publication of certain information before, during and after the procedure; compliance with the deadline for the receipt of tenders; the qualification requirements that may be required of tenderers; the criteria for selecting the most suitable tender.
The legal protection of tenderers is governed by the Remedies Directive.
In the event of a potential breach of procurement law, tenderers may have the decisions of the contracting authority reviewed in accordance with the review procedures.
Both countries have transposed EU requirements into national legislation.
Prevention of corruption
The importance of preventing corruption
There is no legal definition of what corruption actually means in either country.
Corruption is generally understood as an abuse of a personal (confidential) position for its own benefit.
As a rule, this means that such abuse is instigated by those who benefit from it.
Corruption can be a barrier to competition and can cause serious economic losses.
Total corruption-related damage is estimated to amount to 5% of global economic output.
For the EU, these costs are estimated at 1% of the EU's economic output.
Where a contracting authority is involved in a contract, agreements restricting competition or allowing unfair conduct may undermine confidence in the integrity, capacity and overall work of the public administration.
That is why preventing corruption is an essential task for contracting authorities.
Practices and procedures must be monitored, particularly in areas particularly vulnerable to corruption, so that adequate measures can be taken to prevent corruption.
Taking preventive measures and carrying out effective controls can significantly reduce the vulnerability of the decision-making process to corruption.
As a rule, the award of public works contracts is considered to be an area particularly vulnerable to corruption.
Measures to prevent corruption
In Germany, corruption can be prosecuted under criminal and labour law, especially when it comes to active and passive bribery, receiving and offering significant benefits.
The German federal government has issued a manual with recommendations on the prevention of corruption in the federal administration, according to which the following principles are applied throughout the procurement process, apart from risk analysis: The priority of open procurement; the principle of multiple control and transparent decision-making; the separation of the planning, procurement and accounting phases; the precise and complete documentation of the procurement process.
In addition, other measures to prevent corruption have become part of procurement law.
In 2011, the relevant Federal Ministry of Information Technology implemented a system to monitor procurement for the construction of federal roads.
Using this system, data such as estimated costs, the total value of the contract and the involvement of private design offices can be fully recorded and analysed to identify possible inconsistencies.
In the Czech Republic, corruption is prosecuted under criminal law, and the level of labour or civil law is also possible.
The legal requirement for transparency, equal treatment and non-discrimination throughout the procurement process (preparation and implementation) is the most important part of the prevention of corruption.
Anti-corruption measures in this area have been set out by the government in the "Government's anti-corruption strategy 2011-2012," which has been updated to 2013 and 2014.
Some of these measures have been incorporated into the Public Procurement Act and subsequent implementing regulations.
The new legislation contains the following basic provisions: A reduction in the limits for small-scale contracts; an obligation for prior notification by the contracting authority, including the justification for the contract; a ban on limiting the number of tenderers in a restricted procedure and the abolition of the general possibility of limiting the number of tenderers by drawing lots; an obligation to publish contracts, including annexes; an obligation to publish actual and lists of subcontractors.
Results of checks relating to subsequent stages of the procurement procedure
Tender documents
The tender documents shall contain all the information necessary to enable tenderers to prepare their tenders in an appropriate manner.
In particular, the documentation must make it possible to draw up the tender price.
In order to be able to do so, candidates must not be exposed to risks they could not have foreseen.
The tender documents contain, in particular, specifications, as well as requirements for proving the qualifications of the tenderers and criteria for selecting the most advantageous tender.
Requirements of European law and the prevention of corruption
In particular, European law contains requirements relating to technical specifications.
The specifications must restrict competition to the minimum extent possible and all interested parties must have equal access to them.
The specification must be neutral.
Requirements for specific products are allowed on a very limited scale in the procurement documents.
The basic principle of preventing corruption is that the tender documents do not contain requirements that could restrict competition.
Furthermore, the documentation should avoid ambiguities or inaccurate data that create room for speculation and manipulation in the evaluation of bids.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have transposed EU legal provisions into their national legislation.
The basic principle is that specifications should be given in general without reference to a specific product or brand.
The specification must specify the supplies and services required and detail the number of units of works and supplies.
In normal cases, prices should be quoted according to the units of work or supplies (e.g. in CZK / m or in CZK / item).
It is only permissible to define the required works in terms of their functionality in exceptional cases.
In principle, the rule is that specifications must be drawn up in a complete and unambiguous manner.
They must contain precise quantities and must not include works and supplies, the necessity of which is not entirely clear.
Payments in the form of lump sums or according to time worked should be avoided where possible.
These parts of the tender documents must exclude speculation and manipulation based on ambiguous specifications.
The specifications must enable each tenderer to prepare its tender with certainty and without excessive preparatory work.
The invitation to tender shall not be published until the tender documents are complete.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, there is no obligation for contractors to award construction contracts in parts.
The contracting authority may divide the contract into several separate lots where the nature of the subject-matter of the contract so permits.
In Germany, it is required to apply the rule of dividing works into parts, either quantitatively (partial deliveries) or according to specific aspects (specialised parts of the contract).
This allows small and medium-sized entities to participate in the competition, increases the number of potential bidders and makes it more difficult to fix prices.
This type of award is usually considered the most appropriate in terms of providing value for money.
In the Czech Republic, the tender documents must contain evaluation criteria for national and European procurement procedures.
In doing so, the tenderer should be fully and comprehensively informed of the criteria against which its tender will be evaluated.
In Germany, the requirement to indicate the evaluation criteria in the notice applies only to European invitations to tender.
In national procurement procedures it is necessary to include evaluation criteria only in the contract record, which is an internal administrative document.
If the tender documents are prepared by authorised independent experts, German regulations require the exclusion of any information that would help to identify the specific firms, architects or designers involved.
The reason is to prevent individual construction companies from obtaining any additional information or other unacceptable competitive advantages through contacts with these independent experts without the knowledge of the contracting authority.
This is also the reason why independent experts are not allowed to publish tender documents and allow them to be accessible for examination.
The contracting authority is the only point of contact for construction companies.
The Czech Republic does not currently have a similar provision.
In the case of large construction projects, after the conclusion of the original contract, there is almost always a need for additional works or modification of existing works.
The legislation of both countries allows the award of a contract for additional works up to a certain limit to the same contractor, without the need to publish and carry out a new procurement procedure.
The volume of additional works under European and German legislation must not exceed 50% of the original value of the contract.
In addition, the additional work must be necessary and must have the character of a specific component of the main contract.
In the Czech Republic, a stricter limit of 20% is applied for the award of additional works.
This may entail the risk of a greater delay in the execution of the contract and the possible integration of a new entity into an already running project.
If the contract for additional works is awarded to the contractor of the original contract, German law contains a provision that prevents suppliers from arbitrarily increasing the price of modified works or additional works.
The calculations must be made taking into account the prices agreed in the original contract.
In addition to additional works, there may also be minor works, i.e. works that were not needed.
In the Czech Republic, less and more work cannot be compensated for each other.
Main control findings
BHR
Technical specifications and product-neutral call for tender
In rare cases, the specifications referred to national technical requirements or standards, but these have since been replaced by EU standards.
In some cases, the specifications included information requiring a particular brand name, e.g. for the resistance system of public signs, to prevent water penetration or for noise proofing, although this was not necessary.
In some cases, the specifications were so detailed that only one product met the requirements.
Quantity specifications broken down into items
In not a single case of control of procurement procedures did BRH notice a disagreement with the general requirements for the invitation to tender for supplies and services according to the quantity specifications broken down into lots.
Completeness of specifications
In most of the cases audited, tender documents were fully prepared before the call for tenders was launched.
In various cases, however, the documents had to be modified during the procurement procedure when changes in the items or the timetable had to be taken into account.
In isolated cases, the tender documents contained works and supplies, the need for which was uncertain.
One of the tender documents for the demolition works contained almost exclusively such items.
Subdivision of the contract and award criteria
In many cases, the required justification for not splitting deliveries into parts has not been demonstrated.
BRH did not find any shortcomings in the criteria for the evaluation of tenders before the award of the contract.
Inclusion of the names of authorised independent experts
Many of the documents referred to the authorised experts involved.
The names appeared on the opening pages of the documents or on the plans or attached opinions.
In one case, the contracting authority even invited tenderers to contact an explicitly appointed architect with their questions on the tender procedure.
In the case of land construction projects, these cases were mainly found in the tender documents, where the specifications were commissioned by self-employed experts.
The contracting authorities stated that they did not have sufficient staff to examine the tender documents in detail.
The SAO
Technical specifications and product-neutral call for tender
In the case of a public contract for the construction of a house with 12 flats, reference was made directly to specific manufacturers and brands of products.
The contracting authority did not allow the use of other similar types of products, nor did it explain why it required the supply of specific products.
For the construction of the university's facilities, the tender documents contained references to specific commercial firms and specific product labels.
General overview of specifications and sub-specifications
For the construction of certain industrial zones, the contracting authority did not clearly define in the tender documents the quantity and type of works, supplies or services required in the notice of assessment and determined only the enclosed space in cubic metres as a unit of measurement for each building.
Completeness of specifications
During the tendering procedure for the construction of the road circuit, the tender documents for the restricted procedure were amended four times between August and September 2009.
This was due to changes in the project documentation due to an extension of the scope of the contract and a change in the technical conditions.
For the construction of social buildings, the tender documents had defects that forced the creation of additional works (there was a lack of precise focus, insufficient construction technical research, no drawings).
The tender documents for the construction of the large library listed the interior elements only in descriptive terms, without specifying the quality requirements for the products.
The supply of the interior fittings of the building was provided by subcontractors who purchased some above-standard and very expensive equipment.
Parts and criteria for awarding the contract
In the Czech Republic, there is no obligation to award the contract in parts.
An analysis of three large road structures during the construction of the road circuit showed that when they were awarded in an open procedure in parts (separately after selected buildings) it was possible to achieve savings of up to CZK 790 million from the achieved public procurement price of CZK 15,508 million.
For example, when six buildings were awarded as a separate part of the public contract, savings of EUR 62.5 million could be achieved.
A saving of CZK 287.8 million, i.e. also 25%, could have been achieved with the award of 20 bridges as a separate part of the contract.
During the construction of the infrastructure, the contracting authority set out in the tender documents qualification criteria that limited the participation of small suppliers in the tender.
In the terms of the tender for the contractor for one construction of the motorway, the contracting authority requested, among other things, the contractor's practice of carrying out at least three road works with a financial volume of more than CZK 700 million each, completed or put into operation during the last five years.
There are only nine companies in the Czech Republic that meet the condition of completing buildings above CZK 700 million in the last five years.
The public contract for the supply of another motorway construction in an open procedure included six separate works for which the contracting authority requested a minimum annual volume of works of 12 billion in the last three years.
Two thirds of financial volume of road construction works (i.e. €2.5 billion out of €3.7 billion) in the period of 2008-2009 were implemented by 5 suppliers or individually either in form of consortium.
For 46 contracts for a total of 13.2 billion CZK, there were only two or even one bidder.
The SAO pointed out that limiting the market potential can lead to a large increase in costs.
Area of multiwork
In two cases, the contracting authority did not conclude a written contract for the supply of additional works during the construction of the road circuit and did not award additional works to any of the procedures specified in the law.
The value of pre-approved additional works in one case was CZK 1,591.4 million excluding VAT (an increase of 21%) and in the other case CZK 4,135.8 million excluding VAT (an increase of 91%).
The main reason for the additional work was the ambiguous definition of the quantity and type of work required.
For the construction of the university's facilities, the contracting authority did not include in the tender documents certain works and supplies, the necessity of which for the completion and approval of the work was already known when the contract was awarded.
For these works and supplies, he concluded amendments to the contract for the work with the contractor with an increase in the price, in a negotiated procedure without publication, even though the volume of additional works exceeded the national limit of 20% of the value of the original contract.
In connection with the construction of the Palace of Justice, the contracting authority reserved the right to award additional works without a tendering procedure.
During the construction, the contracting authority increased the scope of the contract by CZK 50.5 million.
The unit prices of the additional works, determined without competition, were significantly higher than comparable works carried out by the same contractor.
Other findings
The SAO found on a selected sample of buildings that the average construction cost for the construction of one kilometre of a four-lane motorway in the Czech Republic is CZK 355.0 million.
According to long-term data obtained from the German Ministry of Construction, the average cost in Germany per kilometre of a four-lane motorway is CZK 260.0 million.
This comparison suggests that the acquisition of a motorway in the Czech Republic is more expensive than in Germany.
Summary
The legal provisions relating to the tender documents are almost identical in both countries and are in line with the requirements of European law.
The aim is to ensure full and equal competition and the prevention of corruption.
The specifications must be sufficiently precise to allow bidders to calculate costs and prepare their bids.
In addition, this requirement limits the scope for speculation and the contracting authority is protected against the need to commission additional works.
In Germany, the basic legal provisions on tender documents are complemented by additional rules designed to further reduce the possibilities for restricting competition and for carrying out manipulations.
Although there are no detailed requirements of this kind in the Czech Republic, greater transparency is achieved through the publication of award criteria.
In both countries, the requirements for specifications are mostly met.
In some cases, however, shortcomings have been identified which could lead to a reduction in competition.
At the same time, the rule in force in Germany requiring the division of large construction projects into parts seems appropriate for strengthening the competitive environment and achieving cost-effective results.
The SAO found that in the Czech Republic, the award of contracts for large volume projects without awarding them in parts or dividing them into large volume parts can limit the range of candidates to a small number of large suppliers.
As a result of the strict requirement for bidders' references, which is applied in the Czech Republic for the selection of bidders for road works, public contracts are awarded only to a small number of bidders, leaving small and medium-sized companies with no chance of competing.
The limited number of bidders also increases the risk of price fixing and other illegal agreements.
Regulatory approaches differ between the two countries when it comes to awarding and paying for additional or modified works.
If the value of the additional works under European and German law does not exceed 50% of the value of the original contract, the additional works may be awarded without a tendering procedure or notice.
In the Czech Republic, there is a stricter limit of 20%.
ThissegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
This limit is often exceeded by the contracting authorities.
ThissegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
If the limit of 20% is exceeded, the additional works must be awarded following a new procurement procedure.
By dividing the work into parts, it is possible to avoid the reduction of competition and the subsequent negative consequences.
Determination of the type of procurement procedure with regard to EU limits
By specifying the type of procurement procedure, the contracting authority defines the range of potential suppliers that will be admitted to the competition.
The procuring entity may leave the scope of potential suppliers unlimited or limit the scope according to the specified criteria.
In the case of large project works with a value that reaches or exceeds the limits set by the EU, a European procurement procedure must be conducted.
Requirements of European law and the prevention of corruption
In particular, when choosing the type of procedure, the contracting authority must comply with the requirements of European law relating to the competitive environment and transparency.
If the estimate of the total cost of the contract for the required works reaches or exceeds the current EU limit of €5.0 million ($125 million), an open or restricted procurement procedure at European level is always required.
In exceptional cases, a negotiated procedure or a competitive dialogue is permitted.
If the construction project reaches the EU limit, the contracting authority may, to a limited extent, deviate from the European procurement procedure by launching a national procedure if the total value of the relevant part is below the limit of €1.0 million ($25 million) and the total value of all parts does not exceed 20% of the value of the estimate of the entire contract.
It is not permissible to divide a construction project or deliberately estimate its costs too low in order to avoid a fundamental obligation to launch a European tender.
The choice of the type of governance is very important for preventing corruption, as reducing competition substantially increases the risk of manipulation.
Therefore, a detailed justification of the type of procedure chosen, in particular in relation to the obligation to apply European procurement law, is an important part of the tender documentation.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have fully implemented the EU's legal requirements for procurement procedures and limits in their national legislation.
Different provisions
The Czech Republic has introduced the principle of equal validity of open and restricted procedures also for procurement procedures at national level.
Open and restricted procedures can always be chosen, while other types of proceedings are allowed only if the conditions laid down by law are met.
Unlike the Czech legislation, the relevant German legislation on budgetary law and the principles of prevention of corruption prefers open procedures or a public invitation to participate in the procedure.
Other types of proceedings, including restricted proceedings, are permitted only in exceptional cases where permitted by law.
Such exceptions shall be justified and documented.
The principle of preference for an open procedure is intended to ensure that the restriction of competition is avoided to the maximum extent possible and that the opportunity for manipulation (pre-selected specific candidates) is limited.
In Germany, this principle of public procurement is central to preventing corruption.
A correct estimate of the expected contract price is an important basis for deciding on the appropriate type of procurement procedure.
Contracting authorities in Germany can rely on budgetary and cost databases of public administrations and other organisations to estimate the costs of building projects.
These were compiled on the basis of an analysis of completed construction projects and include, among other things, standard cost values for types of construction, construction units, construction parts and partial construction works.
On the basis of such standard cost values, it is possible to estimate the value of the contract of the planned project or works.
Only a few building administrations in Germany already have a similar database for road construction.
However, the database is expected to be expanded at national level.
Contracting authorities in the Czech Republic do not use any central database or average prices of comparable buildings to determine the expected costs of public works contracts.
In many cases, the determination of costs is based on an estimate by the entity financing the project or on the value of the resources available to that entity.
Standardisation measures are still at an early stage.
On the basis of the anti-corruption programme for the period 2011 to 2012, the relevant ministries are to develop a database of the usual prices of standardised commodities.
The use of a pricing system to determine the expected price of a contract is determined by a new regulation requiring the subject matter of the contract to be determined in detail.
The pricing system should serve as a set of information on construction and assembly work, materials and products and other data to calculate the necessary costs and to establish unit prices.
The Ministry of Transport has issued normative prices for transport works for inspection, which are regularly updated.
The database is designed to determine the price of a project (investment) plan and should play a comparative role in the next stages of the preparation of construction work.
Main BRH control findings
Selection of the procurement procedure
The audited contracting authorities generally awarded contracts in an open procedure or in the form of a public call for participation, thus complying with the requirements of the budget and procurement legislation.
In 2011, public tenders accounted for 59.5% of total federal building projects in the field of building construction.
In road construction, the share was 94.8%.
BRH found that, in some cases, the use of non-public competition was not sufficiently justified.
In the case of blanket control, BRH found, based on information gathered from more than 16,000 proceedings, that limited proceedings have a clear disadvantage compared to public proceedings and public appeals, and limited proceedings also increase the risk of manipulation and corruption.
ThissegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
According to the BRH study, tender prices in restricted and negotiated procedures were between 6% and 10% higher than in public procedures.
Parallel studies by the Ministry of Construction and the Regional Court of Audit confirmed the differences in value for money obtained in public and restricted procedures.
In fact, a study by the Ministry of Construction found that prices in restricted procedures were twice as high as those in public procedures.
Compliance with EU limits
For projects whose total value of the contract was estimated at more than €5 million ($125 million), the audited contracting authorities generally prepared tender plans indicating which works would be awarded in the European tender procedure and which in the national procedure.
According to the procurement plans, the share of works awarded under European rules always exceeded 80% of the total work.
There has been no case of circumvention of the requirement for a European procedure in the form of an excessively low estimate of costs.
However, in some cases, the minimum share for pan-European management was not reached due to a calculation error.
In the course of carrying out the work, some contracting authorities have unlawfully changed the contract award plan.
They did not provide any justification for these changes and did not demonstrate that the minimum share of the European procedure would subsequently be respected.
The share of European procedures according to project accounts was generally lower than the share set out in the procurement plan.
If the expected values of contracts for construction projects exceeded the European limits, the contracting authorities did not apply the European procedure to all individual parts with a value exceeding €1 million ($25 million).
Instead, they awarded the relevant contracts on the basis of a national procedure, without giving any valid reasons for the need to derogate from the requirement.
The SAO
Selection of type of procurement procedure
In the case of several large construction projects (e.g. construction of a road section, facilities for a major sporting event and construction of apartments), the contracting authority divided one project into several parts, although these sub-contracts were of the same or similar nature.
The division reduced the expected value of the contract and the contracts were awarded under a less demanding regime or even outside the statutory regime.
Methods of calculating the estimated price
The price standards applicable to road construction projects have not yet been an effective tool for determining the expected price.
They were based on the prices indicated in the completed procedures, which were, however, burdened by the inefficiencies and excessive profit of the suppliers.
The SAO found that the actual prices for the three parts of the road circuit were about 37% lower than these price standards.
The Ministry of Transport is to update the standards in relation not only to prices from the past, but also in relation to other comparable markets and price developments.
For land buildings, the estimated price was based on the budget of the project carried out by the architects or on an estimate by the investor.
The actual price then differed significantly from the expected price.
The estimated cost of the construction of the large library was twice as high as the contract price, but it increased by CZK 54 million, or 27%, during the construction process.
Similarly, for the construction of the depository of this library, the contract price was 33% lower than the expected price.
In other cases, the price of the successful applicant was higher than the expected price and increased during the implementation process.
For the construction of a large library, it was 400 million CZK, or 22%. For the construction of a judicial palace, the actual price during the construction process increased disproportionately (by 139%) compared to the increase in functional areas (by only 12%).
Summary
Both countries have implemented European provisions on the choice of the type of procurement procedure and respect for European limits in their national legislation, so the respective national regulations of the two countries are almost identical.
However, the principle of public procedures in Germany is stricter than the European rules and the current national rules in the Czech Republic.
Compared to a narrower procurement procedure, a public procedure or a public call for participation gives more competitors the opportunity to participate in the procurement procedure.
These types of procurement procedures generate a large number of bids, which guarantee the best value for money.
