The prevention of corruption is significantly strengthened as the pool of candidates is not limited by decisions taken in advance by the contracting authority, which thus gives room for manipulation and fixing of prices between tenderers.
The studies confirmed the advantages of the German provision on the preference for open procedures or public calls for participation.
Czech law allows contracting authorities to award contracts in an open or restricted procedure.
However, the latest legislation of the Public Procurement Act introduces a ban on contracting authorities from limiting the number of bidders in restricted procedures.
The restricted procedure has now come very close to an open procedure.
In Germany, contracting authorities are largely satisfied with the requirement of preference for an open procedure or a public call for competition, while in the Czech Republic some projects were divided in violation of the law, in order to circumvent the requirement to choose the relevant type of procurement procedure.
However, in the case of some projects carried out in Germany, the European limits were not respected, which resulted in a minimum share not being reached in Europe-wide procedures.
In the Czech Republic, a satisfactory system has not yet been put in place to facilitate the correct calculation of the expected costs of construction works and projects.
Where appropriate, contracting authorities should ensure, through more extensive controls, that the requirements for the selection of the type of procurement procedure and the volume of contracts awarded in European procedures are met or create the conditions necessary to allow a correct calculation of the expected costs.
Preliminary information and notice of initiation of proceedings
In cases where contracts are awarded under a European procedure, the contracting authority shall publish a report on the procurement procedure.
Prior information and contract notices are an important basis for European competition.
Requirements of European law and the prevention of corruption
By means of prior notices, the contracting authority must specify the basic specifications of the intended projects.
Advance notices are required for projects where the total cost estimate is equal to or greater than the European limit and where the contracting authority wishes to reduce the time limit for the submission of tenders in individual procurement procedures.
The contracting authority is obliged to publish a contract notice with detailed information for all public contracts that require a European procedure.
The information shall be binding on the contracting authority after publication.
The notice shall include, inter alia, specifications, time limits for the procedure and for the completion of the contract, specific requirements and selection criteria and their weightings.
A common procurement vocabulary must be used to describe the subject of the contract.
In the EU, preliminary information and contract notices are published in the Official Journal of the EU and on the profile of the contracting authority.
In order to avoid discrimination against foreign candidates, notices at national level should not contain any additional information and should not be published before the text of the notice is sent to the EU Publications Office.
Incomplete or inaccurate information in the advance notice may distort competition or even facilitate manipulation.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have fully implemented the EU provisions on prior information and tender notices in their national legislation.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, preliminary information and notices must also be published in the case of contracts for which a European procedure is not required.
The procurement procedure may not start earlier than one month after the prior notice has been sent.
The publication of prior information may be waived only in exceptional cases.
In Germany, preliminary information is not required in cases where there is no need for a European procedure.
In the case of national proceedings, the public notice must be published on the federal government website and, where appropriate, in the press.
In the case of restricted procedures without competition from the parties, the notice must be published if the estimated contract price is equal to or higher than €25,000 ($625,000).
Advance notices must explain all the planned parts of the project.
This is an important requirement given the number of small orders in Germany.
The division of projects into smaller parts is not as common in the Czech Republic and this measure is considered less important.
Main control findings
BRH
Advance notice
The contracting authorities published a prior notice of projects which included works to be awarded under the European procedure.
In some open procedures, they reduced the time limit, although the number of days between sending the advance notice and sending the contract notice was less than 52 days.
In the case of other summary procedures, the annual deadlines for prior information expired and were due to be renewed.
In many cases, the prior notice did not indicate the expected division of the works into several parts.
Contract notice
Some notices contained codes from the Common Dictionary that deviated from the content of the advance notice or did not specify precisely the work to be carried out.
In isolated cases, the notice at national level included more information, e.g. on delivery dates, or was published before the notice was published in the Official Journal of the EU.
Ex-ante transparency
One contracting authority has hired an external company to publish a national notice.
It published a notice on its platform, the use of which was subject to a fee.
However, the contracting authority did not check whether the company had to comply with the requirement to send a notice to a free internet government portal.
The sample found that only a third of the notices were actually published on the government portal.
The SAO
Contract notice
During the construction of the waste water treatment plant, the contracting authority did not set out in the notice of restricted tender for the contractor the criteria according to which it would reduce the large number of tenders for the evaluation procedure.
The contracting authority was not able to substantiate the method of selection (reduction) of tenders for evaluation.
Previous legislation made it possible to reduce the number of offers.
During the construction of the road circuit, the contracting authority did not define in the notice of initiation of the restricted procedure or in the qualification documents the values against which it would assess the level of compliance with the qualification criteria or the number of candidates to be invited.
The contracting authority assessed the fulfilment of the qualification so that all six candidates in the restricted procedure fulfilled it, but excluded one of them, saying that he fulfilled it only in the last sixth place.
The contracting authority's procedure was not transparent as it did not publish in advance information on what of the qualification data would be evaluated and how.
Publication of calls
In the case of construction works in former military tours, the contracting authority did not publish a written invitation to tender in violation of the law.
Conclusions and recommendations / summary
A two-stage information process with prior information and the publication of a notice of initiation is an important tool for meeting the transparency requirements and strengthening the competitive environment applied in European procedures.
By extending the obligation to publish preliminary information in the Czech Republic also for national procurement procedures and by strengthening this obligation in Germany for restricted procedures, both countries have increased the transparency of procurement procedures and improved the competitive environment.
Selection of candidates in restricted procurement procedures
As opposed to an open procedure, restricted procurement procedures do not allow all potential interested bidders to participate.
In restricted procedures, the contracting authority will first select potential suppliers who will be asked to submit a tender.
Requirements of European law and the prevention of corruption
EU provisions allow a cap on the number of candidates in European restricted procedures.
However, the restricted procedure requires at least five tenderers to be invited to submit a tender and, in the case of a negotiated procedure with publication, at least three potential tenderers must be invited.
In any event, the number of candidates invited must be sufficiently large to guarantee genuine competition.
Selection criteria must be objective and non-discriminatory and must be published in advance.
The selection of potential candidates to be invited is highly susceptible to manipulation, such as the preferences of some of them.
Reducing competition to a few bidders may ease their collusion.
This has a negative impact on the outcome of the proceedings.
Therefore, the contracting authority must take measures to prevent manipulation and ensure that it has effective control to ensure that the procedure is transparent and credible.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
The provisions for selecting candidates are similar in both countries.
In addition to the requirements of the European directive, contracting authorities should replace tenderers who are invited to submit a tender.
Nor is it permissible to restrict competition from a regional point of view.
Contracting entities shall document and justify the selection of tenderers and the selection process.
Different provisions
In Germany, in the case of a restricted procedure, it is required to invite at least three candidates and, in the case of a negotiated procedure, several suitable candidates.
Apart from two cases, the new Czech regulations do not allow limiting the number of candidates approached in the event of a restricted and negotiated procedure.
In Germany, the "four eyes principle" must be respected in the selection of candidates.
While one worker proposes a list of them, another must substantially modify the proposal, for example by removing the proposed candidate or installing another.
This is to avoid a situation in which an individual can definitively determine the number of candidates who will take part in the procedure.
In the absence of such a provision, far-reaching collusion between the parties is permitted.
The list of tenderers to be invited to submit a tender shall be confidential and shall not be divulged.
Authorised independent experts are also obliged to do so.
There is no similar measure in the Czech Republic, nor is there any measure for the confidential treatment of the list of candidates.
Under German provisions, candidates may not be appointed by outside companies, but may be submitted to the contracting authority.
There is no such requirement in the Czech Republic.
Main control findings
BRH
Number of candidates
Apart from a few cases, the requirement to address the minimum number of candidates was met.
An analysis of almost 7,000 contracts for works with a value up to the national limit showed that contracting authorities invited an average of 6.6 potential tenderers in the case of restricted procedures and an average of 4.4 tenderers in the case of direct negotiated procedures.
Change of suppliers and regional restrictions
In particular, in the area of building construction, the contracting authorities did not change the range of tenderers contacted because they had good experience with those with whom they had worked so far.
ThissegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
The contracting authorities argued that, in the case of a non-public procedure, they should ensure that they reach out to tenderers whose suitability they have already ascertained in advance.
Although there was no evidence of illegal regional restrictions, the pool of candidates in closed procedures included a larger proportion of regional applicants than in the case of public procedures.
This was explained by the low level of the contract compared to the public procedure and the high cost of travel for potential bidders.
The principle of cross-checking and proposals by self-employed professionals
Contracting authorities have largely ignored the obligation to amend the list of candidates proposed by self-employed professionals.
In particular, they argued that there was a lack of staff, time pressure or inadequate knowledge of a suitable candidate.
In many cases, the records did not show who proposed the candidate who was asked to take part in the procedure.
An examination of some samples showed that the list of potential tenderers was drawn up by self-employed professionals and was accepted unchanged by the contracting authority.
As a result, contracting authorities did not adequately protect themselves from potential manipulation.
Confidentiality of the treatment of lists of potential candidates
For one contracting authority, a list of candidates for invitations to tender was kept in a database to which all staff had access at any time.
This allowed manipulation or unauthorised disclosure of the names of the expected or accepted bidders, who could then agree on a price.
Documentation
Only in a few cases did the dossier state the reasons for selecting candidates.
The SAO
Criteria for selecting candidates
During the construction of the road circuit, the interested parties did not have information about what of their qualification data would be evaluated and how.
For the action related to the development and modernisation of waterways and ports, the tender notice for participation in the restricted procedure did not set out the criteria for limiting the number of tenderers.
All eight tenderers met the qualification requirements, but this exceeded the number to which the contracting authority limited participation in the procurement procedure.
The Commission then selected the candidates by random selection - drawing lots, which was legal at the time.
This procedure should have been mentioned as a criterion in the contract notice.
Summary
In view of the high risk of manipulation in the selection of the winning bidder in restricted procurement procedures, both countries have put in place special measures to prevent undue restrictions on competition.
German rules restrict such opportunities for manipulation by means of so-called cross-checking (the four-eye principle), by keeping the list of candidates confidential and by prohibiting external companies from making proposals for the selection of candidates.
The Czech rules aim to prevent potential restrictions with the aim of manipulation mainly by an unlimited number of applicants.
However, the audit found that part of those rules had not been applied to a sufficient extent and that an undue restriction of the pool of candidates had not been prevented in some cases.
Contracting authorities are obliged to completely eliminate these deficiencies.
Submission of tenders
The procurement phase, in which potential tenderers can draw up bids on the basis of tender documents received, begins with the publication of information on the procurement procedure and / or a call for tenders.
Until the opening of the envelopes, potential tenderers may submit their tenders to the contracting authority in order to apply for the award of the contract.
Requirements of European law and the prevention of corruption
EU law does not impose specific requirements at this stage of the procurement procedure.
The contracting authority must comply with the national rules governing this stage of the procedure and avoid potential manipulation.
Those provisions relate, in particular, to organisation and control.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
The principle is that tender documents on the basis of which potential suppliers draw up their tenders must always be sent by the contracting authority.
Design offices that are involved in management should not fulfil this role.
The contracting authority must ensure that all candidates have received the same information during this stage of the procurement procedure.
In the event of confusion in the tender documents or if further questions arise, only the contracting authority may supply information to the tenderers.
The design offices responsible for drawing up the tender documents may not provide any information.
Throughout this phase, any contact between the Design Office and the bidders must be avoided.
They must remain anonymous throughout the process as allowing contact with them may adversely affect competition.
Tenders sent by post or submitted directly must be submitted in a sealed envelope.
Tenders submitted electronically must be encoded.
Different provisions
In Germany, the contracting authority may entrust insured suppliers of external services with the printing and distribution of documentation, and their employees must be bound to observe confidentiality.
In Germany, the tenders received must be marked with the date of receipt and handed over directly and immediately in their original state (sealed) to the official in charge of their safekeeping.
This staff member shall not have any interest in the procurement procedure.
Bids received must be locked until the opening date of the envelopes.
In the Czech Republic, the tenders received are marked with a serial number.
The contracting authority shall draw up a report with details of the competition, including the number of the tender, and information on the preparation and receipt of the tender.
There are no specific rules on the custody of offers.
Main control findings
BRH
BRH checked the terms of reference of the administration building.
Submission of tender documents
The audited contracting authorities sent out the tender documents either directly or commissioned an external firm to print and distribute them.
The self-employed did not send any documentation.
Information to be provided to candidates
For the part of the tender documents relating to the questioning and obtaining of information, the contracting authority did not follow the rule for separate planning from the procurement procedure.
In violation of the relevant regulations, he often did not mention the names of his contact staff in the documentation, but rather the staff of the project management department.
Some tender notices even included the names of firms or self-employed persons involved in the preparation of the tender documents.
In rare cases, tenderers who raised questions during the procurement procedure did not receive identical information.
Labelling of offers
For the contracting authorities, all tenders received were stamped with a receipt.
However, in some cases in negotiated procedures, bids were accepted by self-employed professionals, in breach of the one-stop-shop rule for preventing corruption.
Custom of the tenders
Once marketed, not all tenders were passed on directly and without delay to the official assigned to keep them in custodial locked the tenders in a steel cabinet.
For one contracting authority, tenders were temporarily placed in the open compartment of the mailroom, to which all staff had access.
Another contracting authority placed the box in the corridor without constant supervision and part of the tenders thrown into the box remained there overnight.
The opening of the box was wide and unsecured, making it accessible not only to employees.
The SAO
The SAO did not find any shortcomings in this area.
Summary
In both countries, rules are laid down to prevent contacts, particularly between interested companies and design offices that cooperate in the procurement process, throughout the process.
Equal treatment is ensured by sending the same information to all candidates.
Rules to prevent manipulation and corruption are appropriate for the receipt, safekeeping and confidentiality of offers.
In Germany, the requirements are somewhat more developed than in the Czech Republic.
In Germany, the collected inspection records showed breaches of the rules in some areas.
These inconsistencies can easily be corrected.
Opening envelopes
Opening envelopes
During the opening of the envelopes, the tenders which have been closed so far are opened, their contents are read and published.
The period during which a tenderer is bound by its tender shall begin at the end of the period for the submission of the envelopes or their opening and shall end at a date specified by the contracting authority.
Requirements of European law and the prevention of corruption
European legislation does not contain any specific provisions on the opening of envelopes.
However, national laws and regulations contain extensive requirements to protect both bids and competition during this sensitive phase of the entire procurement process.
The principles of "multiple eyes," transparency and credibility of the opening process are intended to prevent manipulation and modification of offers already submitted.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
The opening of the envelopes must be carried out by several persons appointed by the contracting authority.
Compliance with the principle of "multiple eyes" is intended to prevent individuals from covertly manipulating offers.
Candidates may be present when the envelopes are opened.
When opening, all envelopes must be placed in front of the chairman of the commission and are opened one by one.
Late tenders may not be considered.
In order to ensure transparency in the opening of envelopes, this stage, including the conclusions (results), must be recorded in writing, including the names of the tenderers and the prices offered.
The minutes must be read and signed by the group leader.
The minutes may also be signed by the candidates present.
Different provisions
A commission is being set up in the Czech Republic to open the envelopes.
The participation of independent experts shall be permitted.
The members of the committee may also be entrusted with the discussion at subsequent stages of the procedure, i.e. the evaluation of the bids.
In Germany, the opening of envelopes takes place in the presence of the manager and the registrar.
The manager must be an employee of the contracting authority.
The manager and, in the case of building, the registrar may not be involved in the preparatory phase (preparation of the tender documents), may not decide on the award of the contract and may not be a member in the subsequent processing (management) of the contract.
German regulations require that the minutes of the opening of the envelopes contain not only the offer prices, but also all information about discounts and special (unusual) symptoms, such as damaged envelopes when they are received or offers received late.
In the Czech Republic, in addition to the bid prices, the entry must also include an indication of the language of the bid and the signature mandate of the bidder.
German regulations require all parts of opened envelopes to be marked in order to avoid subsequent changes.
Tenders, usually marked with a date perforation, must be kept confidential (secret) at the end of the opening of the envelopes and may only be made available to those who process them further.
Similar measures do not exist in the Czech Republic.
If a single tender is received in the Czech Republic, the envelope may not be opened and the tenderer must be notified immediately.
They do not have this arrangement in Germany, and if a single offer is accepted, it is opened and evaluated.
Main control findings
BRH
Principle of cross-checking
During the opening of the envelopes, the contracting authorities followed the four-eye principle.
The manager and the registrar, who was not assigned another role, were always present.
However, in rare cases, in small negotiated procedures, individuals have opened tenders sent to them without the participation of the contracting authority.
Process of opening envelopes
The opening notes did not always document the progress.
In some cases, late bids were not marked and in some cases signatures were missing.
Indication of offers received
Contracting authorities marked tenders with a date perforation, which was in line with the requirements.
Some of them have mislabelled offers in the past, but changed their practice after BRH's warning.
ThissegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
The SAO
Deadline for receipt of tenders
During road repairs, for one event, the tender was submitted to the contracting authority with a delay of one month, which accepted it.
Summary
The essential parts of the opening provisions in both countries are identical with regard to the principle of "more eyes" and the principle of transparency, thus allowing the risk of corruption and manipulation to be substantially reduced.
The German rules are more complex compared to the Czech Republic, in particular the requirements for precise documentation of the procedure.
The marking, safe keeping and respect for the confidentiality of tenders substantially limit the possibility of subsequent changes to the tenders submitted.
In addition, the staff responsible for opening the envelopes may not carry out any other activities related to the contract to be awarded.
Despite the detection of some irregularities, the findings confirm that the rules for opening envelopes are largely applied.
Assessment and evaluation of tenders
The contracting authority must examine and evaluate the tenders received within a specified period.
At the same time, they must award the contract to the tenderer who best meets the award criteria in the tender.
Requirements of European law and the prevention of corruption
EU law sets out the process of assessing and evaluating bids in several steps:
The first step is to check the suitability of the candidates.
They must be in a good economic and financial position and have the required professional and technical knowledge and skills.
Candidates who have ever been convicted of bribery or defrauding must always be excluded from the procurement procedures.
Bids from non-excluded bidders must be evaluated in terms of numerical accuracy, technical suitability and value for money on the basis of previously published award criteria.
The contracting authority must identify the most economically advantageous tender and, in particular, examine all significantly low price tenders.
The review and evaluation is particularly sensitive and susceptible to manipulation.
For example, bidders may be eliminated without a valid reason, or bids that do not meet the requirements may be kept in competition.
The result of the evaluation may be wrongly influenced by the preference or disadvantage of a tenderer, thereby preventing the selection of the most economically advantageous tender.
The contracting authority must therefore take special measures in the selection of the persons involved in the process and ensure that they meet the requirements of integrity and technical knowledge.
They also need to make sure that the process and the decisions taken are transparent and credible.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
In line with EU requirements, the relevant provisions in both countries require bids to be reviewed and evaluated in successive stages.
During the evaluation, the requirements for supporting candidates "suitability, the evaluation criteria and their weightings set out in the notice or in the tender documents shall not be changed.
The criteria for assessing candidates "suitability can no longer be applied.
Further evaluation can only be carried out on the basis of evaluation criteria.
The tenders submitted must first be examined from a formal point of view, whether they are complete or do not include suspicious elements that could encourage manipulation.
If tenderers are excluded from further examination and evaluation of the tenders submitted by them, they must be informed immediately.
Where the evaluation of the tenders results in the discovery of an excessively low tender price, the tenderer concerned must be given the opportunity to comment on the situation before any rejection of its tender.
The reasons for the decision must be documented in a transparent manner.
Different provisions
In Germany, bidders are required to observe confidentiality and only authorised persons can take part in the evaluation of bids.
The mathematical accuracy of tenders may be reviewed only by the contracting authority.
Only employees who are not involved in the decision-making phase of the selection of the contractor or in the implementation phase of the project may be entrusted with the evaluation.
There are no precise provisions on who can carry out further reviews and evaluations.
When external companies are entrusted with the examination and evaluation of bids, they must be aware that bids are confidential and honestly respect this fact.
They may only submit proposals for decisions to the contracting authority.
In the Czech Republic, tenders must be examined and evaluated by a committee appointed by the contracting authority, which will prepare a report with a proposal for the conclusion of the contract.
However, in both countries, the final decision depends on the contracting authority.
If, in Germany, the documents and declarations required for the submission of tenders are incomplete, the tenderers concerned must then submit the required documents within six days.
ThissegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
If tenderers fail to comply with this obligation, their bids must be excluded.
In the Czech Republic, the contracting authority may decide whether to give the tenderer the opportunity to complete the missing documents.
If a candidate fails to prove it, he or she must be expelled.
In Germany, it is necessary to examine not only the total price, but also the unit prices.
If the price structure of the bid indicates that prices for parts of the overall project were estimated to be disproportionately high or low, bidders must be asked to explain.
If they do not provide an explanation, and if the contracting authority can prove that the tender contains compensatory prices, such tenders must be excluded.
This is intended to prevent bidders from submitting price calculations based on the assumption that substantial quantitative changes will be made to the different parts of the overall project, which may lead to a significant increase in project costs.
In the Czech Republic, the total price of the work is evaluated and the prices of the individual parts are not examined in detail.
Valuation offsets are not a reason to exclude a bid.
In Germany, a contract cannot be awarded to a tenderer who offers an excessively high or low price.
Where one or more tenders differ in price by 10% or more from the price estimated by the contracting authority or from the other tenders, tenderers must prove that their tender has been credibly and convincingly calculated.
If they are unable to do so, they should be excluded by the contracting authority.
In the Czech Republic, a tender can be cancelled after a price review if only bids with disproportionately low prices were received and the bidders did not justify these prices.
If the price of the tender is significantly higher than the contracting authority's estimate, this does not justify a refusal to award the contract to the tenderer in question (unlike German procurement law).
If there is a suspicion in Germany of conduct intended to restrict competition, the contracting authority is obliged to report this immediately to the competent superior authority.
It must decide on the way forward and whether it is appropriate to inform the antitrust authority or the law enforcement authorities.
They must also approve (e.g. if the contract exceeds €50,000 and the contract was not awarded to the lowest bidder) whether the contract will be awarded when the contract is being negotiated or whether the tender will be cancelled.
The Czech rules do not regulate this area.
Main control findings
BRH
Verification of suitability and award criteria
Most of the procurement procedures examined were in line with the relevant requirements.
In the case of some recommendations made by self-employed potential contractors, the contracting authorities themselves did not check the suitability of the tenderers.
ThissegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
In some procurement cases, insufficient documentation was provided as to whether all published evaluation criteria were taken into account by the contracting authority in the selection process or whether the selection decision was based solely on the price criterion.
Selection of interested parties and confidentiality of tenders
Some contracting authorities have used the services of unauthorised persons, such as self-employed persons or employees of building authorities.
They were integrated into the planning or implementation process of construction projects, performing functions that were to be performed exclusively by the contracting authority.
Contracting authorities have tried to justify this by their lack of human resources in the sector.
In some cases, they used self-employed professionals to review offers of services from people who were not formally bound to maintain the confidentiality of offers and to carry out with due care the fulfilment of their obligations under the contract.
As far as contracting authorities are concerned, there was generally no conclusive evidence as to whether tenders in the custody of self-employed professionals were available only to designated persons.
Review and Mathematical Control of Tenders
Some contracting authorities have handed over open envelopes for further examination to self-employed professionals without first carrying out the prescribed tender examination and mathematical examination and without documenting the results of these verifications.
In the event of any intention of manipulation, third parties could make changes to the tenders which could not be identified by the contracting authority.
The examination of the documents revealed a number of cases where unusual elements in the tenders were not adequately labelled or where data on prices and products were missing.
Performance audits
A sample check by one contracting authority revealed that in 75% of procurement cases, the most advantageous tenders deviated by more than 10% from the estimated costs.
One out of 20 tenders was cancelled due to inadequate tender prices.
In many cases, there was a lack of evidence or sufficient documentation on the examination of bids or negotiations with bidders for further clarification.
In many cases, there was also a lack of detailed information on, for example, the unit prices of the most important invoiced items.
Changes in market prices or increases in material costs were often cited as reasons for the deviations.
The contracting authorities argued that it was difficult to identify and prove countervailing prices.
In the case of one call for tenders, made by an entity outside the circle of normal contracting entities, a 66% lower price was offered compared to the initial cost estimate.
No data were recorded on the closer examination of costs or on the identification of the reasons for deviations.
In clarifying this issue, the contracting authority did not find any indication of price dumping for the tenderer to whom the contract might have been awarded.
It was therefore not clear whether the initial cost estimate was excessive or whether the price offered was too low.
The contracting authority later rated the tenderer as a "low cost company" which was not able to meet either the quality requirements or the deadlines.
In another procurement procedure, the contracting authority awarded the contract to a tenderer whose price was 35% higher than the initial cost estimate.
The contracting authority did not cancel the tendering procedure and put forward the unfounded argument that the tenderer could be presumed not to have based its pricing on disproportionately high costs.
Suspected irregularities
One contracting authority was found to have two cases of irregularities, which were reported to the firm responsible for technical supervision.
In one procurement procedure, two tenderers submitted tenders containing identical price calculations, which prompted the contracting authorities to exclude both tenders.
The checks did not show any significant deficiencies in the application of the other provisions.
The SAO
Verification of suitability
For many construction projects, such as university buildings, roads and railways, contracting authorities did not exclude tenderers from participating in the procurement procedure, even though they did not meet the contracting authority's requirements set out in the terms of reference, or did not provide the required qualification, or, conversely, one contracting authority could not substantiate or explain the reason for the exclusion of a candidate who met the requirements.
Evaluation
In the case of the construction of a sewage treatment plant, the individual evaluation criteria of the tender for the contractor were established, but the contracting authority did not respect them during the procedure.
For the railway infrastructure development action, the weighting of the evaluation criteria was not set at the start of the procedure when the contract was awarded, but only at the beginning of the evaluation.
In the case of a road repair project, the assessment of the criterion "shortening of construction time" was not carried out correctly.
For the second and third place bidders, the evaluation incorrectly indicated a greater reduction in the construction time than indicated in their bids.
Documentation of the reasons for selecting a candidate
For the three actions related to housing promotion, the contracting authority did not provide evidence on the basis of which criteria it assessed the tenders received and how it selected the most suitable of them.
Accuracy of the selection of suppliers of small scale contracts related to the construction of a road circuit with an estimated price of up to 500 thousand.
It was not possible to verify CZK, as suppliers were selected only on the basis of telephone consultations about the price and the date of performance, but documents about these negotiations were not prepared.
Summary
In both countries, the rules for the verification and evaluation of offers are in line with EU requirements.
However, they differ with regard to the specific steps of the procedure and the choice of individuals involved in the procedure.
In Germany, unlike the Czech Republic, these steps in the procurement process are governed by stricter and more detailed requirements, especially as regards the application of the cross principle and the protection of bids.
The same applies to the rules for verifying the appropriateness of the prices offered.
In both countries, it was found in some cases that the essential steps of the process in the verification and evaluation of tenders were not documented at all and, if so, only partially.
The lack of control tools and transparency in the decision-making process during the review and evaluation of bids allows manipulation.
Inspections have shown serious deficiencies, such as the transmission of tenders to third parties without prior appropriate verification and documentation of tenders by the contracting authority.
Where contracting entities entrust a third party with the performance of functions related to the examination and evaluation of tenders, appropriate measures and increased control of measures against the increased possibility of manipulation shall be necessary.
In all cases, the German contracting authorities audited did not use the full set of available tools to check the validity of prices and did not deal with their own price structure.
In the Czech Republic, shortcomings were found in the evaluation of the appropriateness of the offers and the use of evaluation criteria.
Completion of the procurement procedure
After examination and evaluation of the tenders, the procurement process is completed by the award of the contract or the cancellation of the procedure.
Requirements of European law and the prevention of corruption
In the case of a European procedure, all tenderers must be informed in advance of the decision to award the contract.
Suppliers shall have the opportunity to review the decision taken within a limited period of 10 or 15 days and to request a review if appropriate.
ThissegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
The time limit of 10 days applies to electronic submissions and 15 days to other submissions.
The contract may not be concluded before the expiry of this period or before the decision of the review procedure.
If the review body rejects the complaint and upholds the decision, candidates and tenderers must be given the opportunity to have the correctness of the application of the procurement law reviewed by an independent court.
This measure ensures that no contracting authority will be able to circumvent the legal protection of suppliers by swiftly implementing procurement decisions.
The contracting authority shall document the progress of the procedure in an appropriate manner.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have implemented management information obligations.
In both countries, the final decision on the outcome of the procurement procedure rests with the contracting authority.
However, it cannot arbitrarily deviate from the results of a properly conducted examination and evaluation.
The contracting authority is to conclude the contract within a specified period.
This is due to the fact that the tenderer is not bound by its tender after the expiry of this period.
The content of the contract must not deviate from the winning bid.
Unsuccessful tenderers should be informed of the result in good time, stating the reason why their bid was not accepted.
The cancellation of a procurement procedure is only allowed if certain conditions are met, for example if the prices in the tenders do not meet the specifications.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, the mandatory waiting time before the conclusion of a contract of 10 or 15 days applies to both European and national procedures.
In Germany, the equivalent provision applies only to European procedures.
In all other procedures in Germany, tenderers cannot be informed of the award of a contract before a decision on its EU provisions and review award.
In the Czech Republic, the tender procedure must be cancelled if only one tender was received or if only one tender met the requirements for its consideration and evaluation.
In Germany, a contract must be awarded even if a single tender has been received, provided that the tender meets the conditions set out in the tender documents and is cost-effective.
Main control findings
BRH
The checks found few irregularities in the completion of the audited procurement processes.
In some European cases, proxy companies have received letters before the notification deadline, which can invalidate the contract.
Also in several cases of open procedures, the letters of authorisation contained modifications incompatible with the content of the tender, such as new deadlines.
In some cases, contracting authorities did not meet the deadlines for awarding the contract.
In one open procedure, the contracting authority selected the second most advantageous tender because the tenderer offering a price more than 50% lower no longer wished to fulfil the contract at the price originally offered.
The SAO
In one case, during the reconstruction and construction of the university, the contracting authority did not conclude a contract with the contractor for the work in accordance with the draft contract contained in the tender of the selected tenderer.
For some road repair and maintenance operations, the construction of a motorway and a road ring road, contracts were concluded later than stipulated by law.
In the case of construction works in former military tours, the contracting authority sent a notice of the selection of the most suitable tender to all tenderers only after the legal deadline.
Summary
Both countries have implemented EU rules on the termination of procurement procedures.
The legal protection of unsuccessful candidates helps to reduce corruption.
The Czech Public Procurement Act includes an institute of legal protection even in cases of contracts below EU limits.
In both countries, it was found that the limited time limit for concluding the contract was exceeded.
This can cause adverse economic consequences and increase the risk of manipulation.
In the future, contracting authorities should eliminate such deficiencies in the conclusion of the procurement procedure.
Documentation, notification, information obligations
The procurement process, with all its different stages, relevant findings and decisions, including the relevant justifications, shall be documented in a transparent manner.
The completion of the procurement procedure at European level must be made public.
Data on procurement procedures are summarised in EU statistics.
Requirements of European law and the prevention of corruption
The obligation to document procurement procedures is in line with the fundamental requirement of the principle of transparency, which applies at EU level.
Only proper and time-relevant documentation of decision-making and justification guarantees transparency in the procurement process and, above all, guarantees effective legal protection of the interests of each tenderer in cases where the value of the contract exceeds European limits.
Adequate documentation is also an essential part of anti-corruption measures, as the obligation to provide detailed reasons for individual decisions and their merits significantly reduces the scope for manipulation.
In European procurement cases, the contracting authority must notify the EU's Office of Official Publications within 48 days of the date of award of the contract.
The Authority publishes the notification in the Official Journal of the EU, which also meets the transparency requirement.
The contracting authority must summarise the essential information on its procurement procedures and communicate it to the European Commission through the relevant national ministries.
On this basis, the European Commission publishes annual statistics which provide an overview of the development of the single market in the field of construction.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have fully transposed the provisions of the European law on documentation and notification obligations into their national legislation.
The provisions on documentation and notification for European statistics are almost identical in both countries.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, the obligation to notify the award of a contract applies not only to European procedures, but also to national procurement procedures.
The notification period is only 15 days from the conclusion of the contract.
In Germany, the obligation to notify procurement procedures, in addition to those that are mandatory under European law, applies only to limited procedures exceeding the limit of €25,000 ($625,000) and direct procedures exceeding the limit of €15,000 ($375,000), including VAT.
Only interested parties shall be informed below this limit.
Main control findings
BRH
Documentation
A large number of entries have not been fully documented.
The notification did not contain all the necessary information, such as the reasons for the supply specifications and national rules.
In the case of one procedure, the contracting authority did not explain why it had concluded a contract with a tenderer whose tender did not contain the mandatory requirements.
In several cases some of the required records were also missing.
Publication of the contract award
In the case of some European procedures, the contracting authority erred in the publication of the notice.
In the case of several awards, there was no evidence of publication of the notice.
Obligation to notify
The statistically proven files included the required minimum of information and were created and sent on time.
Preliminary transparency
In most cases, the contracting authority has published information on the outcome of the contract.
In the case of one contracting authority, information about the contract was not published due to a communication error.
It took the sponsor six months to notice the error.
As a result, the outcome of the proceedings was not made public during that period.
The SAO
Documentation
For events related to the organisation of a major sporting event, significant deficiencies were found in the tender documentation, such as incorrect tender price data in the minutes of the evaluation committee meeting.
In addition, some of the opening documents were not signed.
Publication of the contract award
In preparation for repairs and maintenance of roads, construction of the motorway and the road circuit, the contract award notices were sent after the deadline or were not published.
Summary
The provisions on documentation and notification and notification obligations cover European requirements and are almost identical in both countries.
This generally ensures the transparency of procurement procedures and final selection.
The Czech reporting obligations are somewhat stricter compared to the German ones.
The checks revealed shortcomings that significantly reduced the value of documentation as a means of preventing corruption.
ThissegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
Contracting entities should continue to enhance the transparency of their procurement procedures and fully comply with the notification and notification requirements.
Summary assessment and recommendations
The parallel audits of the SAO and BRH focused on two areas that are essential for the public procurement process in both the Czech Republic and Germany, namely the application of EU procurement law and the prevention of corruption.
EU legislation provides an opportunity for international competition within the EU with a view to creating a single European market.
Measures to prevent corruption are not only aimed at avoiding or at least reducing corruption, but also at preventing financial damage and possible loss of confidence in the integrity of the government.
The audit findings of the two audit institutions - the SAO and BRH - with regard to the application of the procurement law of the two countries and the comparison of its relevant provisions give an internal view of the common and different elements that can help both countries in the further development and application of this law.
Application of EU procurement law
Both countries have transposed EU public procurement law into their national legislation.
In both countries, there is thus an important common basis for public procurement and the fulfilment of the basic requirements in the area of: Competition; equal treatment of candidates; transparency of procurement procedures.
Contracting authorities reinforce these requirements in part through very extensive measures.
The Czech Republic has extended the provisions on public procurement requiring European procedures to procedures that are below EU limits and are therefore governed by national legislation.
However, inspections have shown that contracting authorities do not always fully comply with all relevant provisions of EU requirements.
German contracting authorities should continue to improve the quality of their procurement procedures in order to eliminate these irregularities, taking into account the implementation of EU requirements.
In the Czech Republic, checks have identified risks affecting economy and value for money.
These include, in particular, the incomplete definition of the subject-matter of the contract, which resulted in additional works, which were often awarded in breach of the law, the lack of definition of the expected price, and the required qualifications, which narrowed the potential pool of bidders and thus reduced competition, especially in the field of transport works.
Prevention of corruption
The joint checks were not intended to detect cases of corruption.
The reason was that both BRH and the SAO did not have sufficient tools to do so.
However, they can examine whether the competent authorities of both countries are taking measures to reduce corruption and manipulation in public procurement and how these measures are applied.
When it comes to preventing corruption, the two countries have focused on different aspects.
In particular, the German provisions provide for broad and unrestricted competition, transparency and controls as a central element in preventing corruption.
In particular, the Czech regulations aim at strengthening the transparency of procurement procedures.
BRH
In Germany, there are general principles for preventing corruption that apply to the entire federal administration.
These principles have also been incorporated into public procurement requirements and have been adopted to meet the specific needs of public procurement.
The relevant provisions specify, sometimes with great precision, how to meet the requirements for preventing corruption during the procurement procedure and for selecting the persons involved in it.
In BRH's view, the principle of preference for open procurement procedures and calls for participation is a particularly appropriate instrument to substantially reduce the risk of corruption in public procurement, while limiting competition increases the risk of manipulation.
Risk analysis and adequate controls (in particular the principle of cross-checking), transparency requirements, tender documentation and various organisational measures are also effective mechanisms to prevent corruption and manipulation.
BRH therefore does not feel the need for substantial changes to the current provisions that public institutions have to comply with when awarding public contracts.
Anti-corruption measures are widely applied in practice.
The discrepancies found in some areas highlighted weaknesses in compliance with some requirements.
Therefore, contracting authorities should provide further training to their staff, familiarising them with anti-corruption requirements and further raising their awareness in this area.
A significant part of the weaknesses identified relate to activities delegated by the building administration to private entrepreneurs.
However, the lack of adequate controls on the delegation of powers increases the risk of misuse.
Activities susceptible to manipulation and corruption should therefore be transferred to third parties only if the administration can guarantee a sufficient level of protection against unauthorised interference.
The SAO
Anti-corruption strategies have been adopted at government level in the Czech Republic, which also have a significant impact on public procurement.
The SAO's audit activities took place during a period when one of the most important parts of this strategy was implemented, namely a significant change in the Public Procurement Act and the adoption of subsequent implementing regulations.
The strategy adopted and the new legislation can be effective steps to improve the situation.
For example, under the new regulations, the contracting authority must justify the effectiveness of the public contract, the determination of the estimated value of the public contract, the adequacy of the requirements for qualifications, the establishment of basic evaluation criteria and the way tenders are evaluated, and properly document and publish everything.
Recommendations
Overall, both the SAO and the BRH have a favourable opinion of the ways in which the EU requirements are implemented.
Their wide application in practice has confirmed that the EU's requirements for competition, equal treatment and transparency provide an appropriate basis for public procurement within the EU.
The supranational model, established in the EU, can also serve as a basis for similar projects aimed at facilitating and promoting international competition.
Taking into account the great potential for material and intangible damage resulting from manipulation and corruption in connection with public procurement, both BRH and the SAO insist that it is the responsibility of contracting authorities to continue measures against corruption and manipulation and to improve them where possible.
In particular, organisational structures and procurement processes should be continuously monitored for weaknesses and risks in order to identify and respond appropriately to the need for corrective action.
Compared to the practice in Germany, it appeared that the share of open tender procedures in the Czech Republic, which give room to competition and substantially reduce the possibility of corrupt behaviour, should increase.
The award of contracts in parts can also have a positive impact on the price achieved in cases where it is permissible and appropriate for the contracting authority. A higher number of bidders puts pressure on the price achieved.
The organisation of the procurement procedure also has an impact on the conduct of the procurement procedure.
In Germany, it is provided by special state organisations, while in the Czech Republic, with the exception of transport structures, it is organised by the central authorities themselves or through contractual organisations.
Both the SAO and BRH wish to further develop knowledge in their work where it is appropriate, and to address it with their parliament and government beyond their joint project.
In addition, they believe that the principles of procurement law and their implementation, as described in this report, will also be of interest at the level of the International Organisation of Supreme Audit Institutions (INTOSAI), as these principles and findings are of general importance for the effective fight against corruption and for ensuring that public contracts are awarded under a regime of well-functioning and fair competition and that value for money is achieved.
